<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg/220px-N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg/330px-N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg/440px-N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg 2x" data-file-width="832" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C2%B0_9_de_la_place_dans_le_centre_ville_de_Langres.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>N° 9 de la place dans le centre ville de Langres</i>: in the background on the right side the birthplace of Denis Diderot</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg/170px-Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="326" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg/255px-Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg/340px-Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1310" data-file-height="2515" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Langres_-_Denis_Diderot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Denis Diderot in the city of <a href="/wiki/Langres" title="Langres">Langres</a>, his birthplace</div></div></div>
<p>Denis Diderot was born in <a href="/wiki/Langres" title="Langres">Langres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Champagne" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of Champagne">Champagne</a>. His parents were <a href="/wiki/Didier_Diderot" title="Didier Diderot">Didier Diderot</a> (1685–1759), a <a href="/wiki/Cutlery" title="Cutlery">cutler</a>, <i>maître coutelier</i>, and Angélique Vigneron (1677–1748). Three of five siblings survived to adulthood, Denise Diderot (1715–1797) and their youngest brother Pierre-Didier Diderot (1722–1787), and finally their sister Angélique Diderot (1720–1749). According to Arthur McCandless Wilson, Denis Diderot greatly admired his sister Denise, sometimes referring to her as "a female <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diderot began his formal education at a <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> college in Langres, earning a Master of Arts degree in philosophy in 1732. He then entered the <a href="/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge_d%27Harcourt" class="mw-redirect" title="Collège d&#39;Harcourt">Collège d'Harcourt</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>. He abandoned the idea of entering the clergy in 1735,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and instead decided to study at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Law_Faculty" title="Paris Law Faculty">Paris Law Faculty</a>. His study of law was short-lived however and in the early 1740s, he decided to become a writer and translator.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Because of his refusal to enter one of the <a href="/wiki/Profession" title="Profession">learned professions</a>, he was disowned by his father, and for the next ten years he lived a <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">bohemian</a> existence.<sup id="cite_ref-Arthur_Wilson_1972_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arthur_Wilson_1972-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1742, he befriended <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, whom he met while watching games of chess and drinking coffee at the <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_de_la_R%C3%A9gence" title="Café de la Régence">Café de la Régence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 1743, he further alienated his father by marrying <a href="/wiki/Anne-Antoinette_Diderot" title="Anne-Antoinette Diderot">Antoinette Champion</a> (1710–1796), a devout Roman Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The match was considered inappropriate due to Champion's low social standing, poor education, fatherless status, and lack of a dowry. She was about three years older than Diderot. The marriage, in October 1743, produced one surviving child, a girl. Her name was Angélique, named after both Diderot's dead mother and sister. The death of his sister, a nun, in her convent may have affected Diderot's opinion of religion. She is assumed to have been the inspiration for his novel about a nun, <i><a href="/wiki/La_Religieuse_(novel)" title="La Religieuse (novel)">La Religieuse</a></i>, in which he depicts a woman who is forced to enter a convent where she suffers at the hands of the other nuns in the community.<sup id="cite_ref-Arthur_Wilson_1972_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arthur_Wilson_1972-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diderot had affairs with Mlle. Babuti (who would marry <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Greuze" title="Jean-Baptiste Greuze">Greuze</a>), <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_de_Puisieux" title="Madeleine de Puisieux">Madeleine de Puisieux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louise-Henriette_Volland" class="mw-redirect" title="Louise-Henriette Volland">Sophie Volland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mme_de_Maux" title="Mme de Maux">Mme de Maux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_675-6_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_675-6-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> His letters to Sophie Volland are known for their candor and are regarded to be "among the literary treasures of the eighteenth century".<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_675_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_675-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_works">Early works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Diderot's earliest works included a translation of <a href="/wiki/Temple_Stanyan" title="Temple Stanyan">Temple Stanyan</a>'s <i>History of Greece</i> (1743); with two colleagues, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Vincent_Toussaint" title="François-Vincent Toussaint">François-Vincent Toussaint</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine_Eidous" title="Marc-Antoine Eidous">Marc-Antoine Eidous</a>, he produced a translation of <a href="/wiki/Robert_James_(physician)" title="Robert James (physician)">Robert James</a>'s <i>Medicinal Dictionary</i> (1746–1748).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 1745, he published a translation of <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley-Cooper,_3rd_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 3rd Earl of Shaftesbury">Shaftesbury</a>'s <i>Inquiry Concerning Virtue and Merit</i>, to which he had added his own "reflections".<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_625_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_625-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_Thoughts"><i>Philosophical Thoughts</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Philosophical Thoughts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Thoughts" title="Philosophical Thoughts">Philosophical Thoughts</a></div>
<p>In 1746, Diderot wrote his first original work: the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Thoughts" title="Philosophical Thoughts">Philosophical Thoughts</a></i> (French:<i>Pensées philosophiques</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_27_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_27-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In this book, Diderot argued for a reconciliation of reason with feeling so as to establish harmony. According to Diderot, without feeling there is a detrimental effect on virtue, and no possibility of creating sublime work. However, since feeling without discipline can be destructive, reason is necessary to control feeling.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_625_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_625-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time Diderot wrote this book he was a deist. Hence there is a defense of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a> in this book, and some arguments against atheism.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_625_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_625-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The book also contains criticism of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_626_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_626-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_Skeptic.27s_Walk"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Skeptic's_Walk"><i>The Skeptic's Walk</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Skeptic&#039;s Walk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Skeptic%27s_Walk" title="The Skeptic&#39;s Walk">The Skeptic's Walk</a></div>
<p>In 1747, Diderot wrote <i>The Skeptic's Walk</i> (French:<i>Promenade du sceptique</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Fellows_1977_41_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fellows_1977_41-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> in which a deist, an atheist, and a pantheist have a dialogue on the nature of divinity. The deist gives the <a href="/wiki/Teleological_argument" title="Teleological argument">argument from design</a>. The atheist says that the universe is better explained by physics, chemistry, matter, and motion. The pantheist says that the cosmic unity of mind and matter, which are co-eternal and comprise the universe, is God. This work remained unpublished till 1830. The local police—warned by the priests of another attack on Christianity—either seized the manuscript, or authorities forced Diderot give an undertaking that he would not publish this work, according to different versions of what happened.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_626_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_626-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Indiscreet_Jewels"><i>The Indiscreet Jewels</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Indiscreet Jewels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Indiscreet_Jewels" title="The Indiscreet Jewels">The Indiscreet Jewels</a></div>
<p>In 1748, Diderot needed to raise money on short notice. He had become a father through his wife, and his mistress <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_de_Puisieux" title="Madeleine de Puisieux">Mme. de Puisieux</a> was making financial demands from him. At this time, Diderot had stated to Mme. de Puisieux that writing a novel was a trivial task, whereupon she challenged him to write a novel. In response, Diderot wrote his novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Indiscreet_Jewels" title="The Indiscreet Jewels">The Indiscreet Jewels</a></i> (French:<i>Les bijoux indiscrets</i>). The book is about the magical ring of a Sultan which induces any woman's "discreet jewels"<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_44_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_44-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> to confess their sexual experiences when the ring is pointed at them.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In all, the ring is pointed at thirty different women in the book—usually at a dinner or a social meeting—with the Sultan typically being visible to the woman.<sup id="cite_ref-RousseauPorter1990_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RousseauPorter1990-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_627_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_627-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> However, since the ring has the additional property of making its owner invisible when required, a few of the sexual experiences recounted are through direct observation with the Sultan making himself invisible and placing his person in the unsuspecting woman's boudoir.<sup id="cite_ref-RousseauPorter1990_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RousseauPorter1990-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides the bawdiness there are several digressions into philosophy, music, and literature in the book. In one such philosophical digression, the Sultan has a dream in which he sees a child named "Experiment" growing bigger and stronger till it demolishes an ancient temple named "Hypothesis". The book proved to be lucrative for Diderot even though it could only be sold clandestinely. It is Diderot's most published work.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_627_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_627-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The book is believed to be an imitation of <a href="/wiki/The_Sofa:_A_Moral_Tale" title="The Sofa: A Moral Tale"><i>Le Sopha</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_627_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_627-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_work">Scientific work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scientific work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diderot would keep writing on science in a desultory way all his life. The scientific work of which he was most proud was <i>Memoires sur differents sujets de mathematique</i> (1748). This work contains original ideas on <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a>, tension, <a href="/wiki/Air_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a>, and "a project for a new organ" which could be played by all. Some of Diderot's scientific works were applauded by contemporary publications of his time like <i>The Gentleman's Magazine</i>, the <i>Journal des savants</i>; and the Jesuit publication <i>Journal de Trevoux,</i> which invited more such work: "on the part of a man as clever and able as M. Diderot seems to be, of whom we should also observe that his style is as elegant, trenchant, and unaffected as it is lively and ingenious."<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_627_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_627-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the unity of nature, Diderot wrote, "Without the idea of the whole, philosophy is no more," and, "Everything changes; everything passes; nothing remains the but the whole." He wrote of the temporal nature of molecules, and rejected <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/embo%C3%AEtement" class="extiw" title="wikt:emboîtement">emboîtement</a></i>, the view that organisms are pre-formed in an infinite regression of non-changing germs. He saw minerals and species as part of a spectrum, and was fascinated with <a href="/wiki/Hermaphroditism" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermaphroditism">hermaphroditism</a>. His answer to the universal attraction in <a href="/wiki/Corpuscularianism" title="Corpuscularianism">corpuscular</a> physics models was universal elasticity. His view of nature's flexibility foreshadows the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, but it is not <a href="/wiki/Darwinistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Darwinistic">Darwinistic</a> in a strict sense.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_on_the_Blind"><i>Letter on the Blind</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Letter on the Blind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diderot's celebrated <i><a href="/wiki/Letter_on_the_Blind" title="Letter on the Blind">Letter on the Blind</a></i> (<i>Lettre sur les aveugles à l'usage de ceux qui voient</i>) (1749) introduced him to the world as an original thinker.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley1911_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley1911-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The subject is a discussion of the interrelation between man's reason and the <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> acquired through perception (the <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">five senses</a>). The title of his book also evoked some ironic doubt about who exactly were "the blind" under discussion. In the essay, blind English mathematician <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Saunderson" title="Nicholas Saunderson">Nicholas Saunderson</a><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> argues that, since knowledge derives from the senses, mathematics is the only form of knowledge that both he and a sighted person can agree on. It is suggested that the blind could be taught to read through their sense of touch. (A later essay, <i>Lettre sur les sourds et muets</i>, considered the case of a similar deprivation in the <a href="/wiki/Deaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Deaf">deaf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speech_disorder" title="Speech disorder">mute</a>.) According to <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Israel" title="Jonathan Israel">Jonathan Israel</a>, what makes the <i>Lettre sur les aveugles</i> so remarkable, however, is its distinct, if undeveloped, presentation of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variability" title="Genetic variability">variation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>This powerful essay, for which <a href="/wiki/La_Mettrie" class="mw-redirect" title="La Mettrie">La Mettrie</a> expressed warm appreciation in 1751, revolves around a remarkable deathbed scene in which a dying blind philosopher, Saunderson, rejects the arguments of a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a> clergyman who endeavours to win him round to a belief in a <a href="/wiki/Divine_Providence" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine Providence">providential</a> God during his last hours. Saunderson's arguments are those of a neo-<a href="/wiki/Spinoza" class="mw-redirect" title="Spinoza">Spinozist</a> <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">Naturalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatalist">fatalist</a>, using a sophisticated notion of the <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">self-generation</a> and natural evolution of species without Creation or supernatural intervention. The notion of <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">"thinking matter"</a> is upheld and the "<a href="/wiki/Argument_from_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Argument from design">argument from design</a>" discarded (following La Mettrie) as hollow and unconvincing. The work appeared anonymously in Paris in June 1749, and was vigorously suppressed by the authorities. Diderot, who had been under police surveillance since 1747, was swiftly identified as the author, had his manuscripts confiscated, and was imprisoned for some months, under a <i><a href="/wiki/Lettre_de_cachet" title="Lettre de cachet">lettre de cachet</a></i>, on the outskirts of Paris, in the dungeons at <a href="/wiki/Vincennes" title="Vincennes">Vincennes</a> where he was visited almost daily by <a href="/wiki/Rousseau" class="mw-redirect" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a>, at the time his closest and most assiduous ally.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Voltaire wrote an enthusiastic letter to Diderot commending the <i>Lettre</i> and stating that he had held Diderot in high regard for a long time to which Diderot had sent a warm response. Soon after this, Diderot was arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Science historian <a href="/wiki/Conway_Zirkle" title="Conway Zirkle">Conway Zirkle</a> has written that Diderot was an <a href="/wiki/History_of_evolutionary_thought" title="History of evolutionary thought">early evolutionary thinker</a> and noted that his passage that described natural selection was "so clear and accurate that it almost seems that we would be forced to accept his conclusions as a logical necessity even in the absence of the evidence collected since his time."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Incarceration_and_release">Incarceration and release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Incarceration and release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Angered by public resentment over the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1748)" title="Treaty of Aix-la-Chapelle (1748)">Peace of Aix-la-Chapelle</a>, the government started incarcerating many of its critics. It was decided at this time to rein in Diderot. On 23 July 1749, the governor of the <a href="/wiki/Vincennes" title="Vincennes">Vincennes</a> fortress instructed the police to incarcerate Diderot, and the next day he was arrested and placed in solitary confinement in the Vincennes. He had been permitted to retain one book that he had in his possession at the time of his arrest, <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i>, which he read during his incarceration. He wrote notes and annotations on the book, using a toothpick as a pen, and ink that he made by scraping slate from the walls and mixing it with wine.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_630_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_630-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1749, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89milie_du_Ch%C3%A2telet" title="Émilie du Châtelet">Mme du Chatelet</a>, presumably at <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>'s behest, wrote to the governor of Vincennes, who was her relative, pleading that Diderot be lodged more comfortably while jailed. The governor then offered Diderot access to the great halls of the Vincennes castle and the freedom to receive books and visitors providing he would write a document of submission.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_630_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_630-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> On 13 August 1749, Diderot wrote to the governor:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I admit to you...that the <i>Pensees</i>, the <i>Bijoux</i>, and the <i>Lettre sur les aveugles</i> are debaucheries of the mind that escaped from me; but I can...promise you on my honor (and I do have honor) that they will be the last, and that they are the only ones...As for those who have taken part in the publication of these works, nothing will be hidden from you. I shall depose verbally, in the depths[secrecy] of your heart, the names both of the publishers and the printers.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_631_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_631-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>On 20 August, Diderot was lodged in a comfortable room in the Vincennes, allowed to meet visitors, and to walk in the gardens of the Vincennes. On 23 August, Diderot signed another letter promising to never leave the Vincennes without permission.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_631_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_631-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On 3 November 1749, Diderot was released from the Vincennes.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_632_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_632-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, in 1750, he released the prospectus for the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9die,_ou_dictionnaire_raisonn%C3%A9_des_sciences,_des_arts_et_des_m%C3%A9tiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Encyclopédie, ou dictionnaire raisonné des sciences, des arts et des métiers">Encyclopédie</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Encyclop.C3.A9die"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Encyclopédie">Encyclopédie</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Encyclopédie">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9die" title="Encyclopédie">Encyclopédie</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genesis">Genesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Genesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg/220px-Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="360" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg/330px-Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg/440px-Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3133" data-file-height="5120" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Encyclopedie_de_D%27Alembert_et_Diderot_-_Premiere_Page_-_ENC_1-NA5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9die" title="Encyclopédie">Encyclopédie</a></i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_le_Breton" title="André le Breton">André le Breton</a>, a bookseller and printer, approached Diderot with a project for the publication of a translation of <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_Chambers" title="Ephraim Chambers">Ephraim Chambers</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Cyclopaedia,_or_Universal_Dictionary_of_Arts_and_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclopaedia, or Universal Dictionary of Arts and Sciences">Cyclopaedia, or Universal Dictionary of Arts and Sciences</a></i> into French, first undertaken by the Englishman <a href="/wiki/John_Mills_(encyclopedist)" title="John Mills (encyclopedist)">John Mills</a>, and followed by the German <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Sellius" title="Gottfried Sellius">Gottfried Sellius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Diderot accepted the proposal, and transformed it. He persuaded Le Breton to publish a new work, which would consolidate ideas and knowledge from the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Letters" title="Republic of Letters">Republic of Letters</a>. The publishers found capital for a larger enterprise than they had first planned. <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d&#39;Alembert">Jean le Rond d'Alembert</a> was persuaded to become Diderot's colleague, and permission was procured from the government.
</p><p>In 1750 an elaborate prospectus announced the project, and in 1751 the first volume was published.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This work was unorthodox and advanced for the time. Diderot stated that "An encyclopedia ought to make good the failure to execute such a project hitherto, and should encompass not only the fields already covered by the academies, but each and every branch of human knowledge." Comprehensive knowledge will give "the power to change men's common way of thinking."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The work combined scholarship with information on trades. Diderot emphasized the abundance of knowledge within each subject area. Everyone would benefit from these insights.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diderot's work, however, was mired in controversy from the beginning; the project was suspended by the courts in 1752. Just as the second volume was completed accusations arose regarding seditious content, concerning the editor's entries on religion and natural law. Diderot was detained and his house was searched for manuscripts for subsequent articles: but the search proved fruitless as no manuscripts could be found. They were hidden in the house of an unlikely confederate—<a href="/wiki/Guillaume-Chr%C3%A9tien_de_Lamoignon_de_Malesherbes" title="Guillaume-Chrétien de Lamoignon de Malesherbes">Chretien de Lamoignon Malesherbes</a>, who originally ordered the search. Although Malesherbes was a staunch absolutist, and loyal to the monarchy—he was sympathetic to the literary project. Along with his support, and that of other well-placed influential confederates, the project resumed. Diderot returned to his efforts only to be constantly embroiled in controversy.
</p><p>These twenty years were to Diderot not merely a time of incessant drudgery, but harassing persecution and desertion of friends. The ecclesiastical party detested the <i>Encyclopédie</i>, in which they saw a rising stronghold for their philosophic enemies. By 1757 they could endure it no longer—the subscribers had grown from 2,000 to 4,000, a measure of the growth of the work in popular influence and power.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Diderot wanted the <i>Encyclopédie</i> to give all the knowledge of the world to the people of France. However, the <i>Encyclopédie</i> threatened the governing social classes of France (aristocracy) because it took for granted the justice of <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">freedom of thought</a>, and the value of science and industry.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> It asserted the doctrine that the main concern of the nation's government ought to be the nation's common people. It was believed that the <i>Encyclopédie</i> was the work of an organized band of conspirators against society, and that the dangerous ideas they held were made truly formidable by their open publication. In 1759, the <i>Encyclopédie</i> was formally suppressed.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The decree did not stop the work, which went on, but its difficulties increased by the necessity of being clandestine. <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d&#39;Alembert">Jean le Rond d'Alembert</a> withdrew from the enterprise and other powerful colleagues, including <a href="/wiki/Anne_Robert_Jacques_Turgot,_Baron_de_Laune" class="mw-redirect" title="Anne Robert Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune">Anne Robert Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune</a>, declined to contribute further to a book which had acquired a bad reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley1911_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley1911-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Diderot.27s_contribution"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Diderot's_contribution">Diderot's contribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Diderot&#039;s contribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diderot was left to finish the task as best he could. He wrote 7,000 articles,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> some very slight, but many of them laborious, comprehensive, and long. He damaged his eyesight correcting proofs and editing the manuscripts of less competent contributors. He spent his days at workshops, mastering manufacturing processes, and his nights writing what he had learned during the day. He was incessantly harassed by threats of police raids. The last copies of the first volume were issued in 1765.
</p><p>In 1764, when his immense work was drawing to an end, he encountered a crowning mortification: he discovered that the bookseller, Le Breton, fearing the government's displeasure, had struck out from the proof sheets, after they had left Diderot's hands, all passages that he considered too dangerous. "He and his printing-house overseer," writes Furbank, "had worked in complete secrecy, and had moreover deliberately destroyed the author's original manuscript so that the damage could not be repaired."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The monument to which Diderot had given the labor of twenty long and oppressive years was irreparably mutilated and defaced.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It was 12 years, in 1772, before the subscribers received the final 28 folio volumes of the <i>Encyclopédie, ou dictionnaire raisonné des sciences, des arts et des métiers</i> since the first volume had been published.
</p><p>When Diderot's work on the <i>Encyclopédie</i> project came to a end in 1765, he expressed concerns to his friends that the twenty-five years he had spent on the project had been wasted.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mature_works">Mature works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mature works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the <i>Encyclopédie</i> was Diderot's most monumental product, he was the author of many other works that sowed nearly every intellectual field with new and creative ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Diderot's writing ranges from a graceful trifle like the <i>Regrets sur ma vieille robe de chambre</i> (<i>Regrets for my Old Dressing Gown</i>) up to the heady <i><a href="/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_Dream" class="mw-redirect" title="D&#39;Alembert&#39;s Dream">D'Alembert's Dream</a></i> (<i>Le Rêve de d'Alembert</i>) (composed 1769), a philosophical dialogue in which he plunges into the depths of the controversy as to the ultimate constitution of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Meaning_of_life_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Meaning of life (philosophy)">meaning of life</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Jacques_le_fataliste" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacques le fataliste">Jacques le fataliste</a></i> (written in 1773, but not published until 1792 in German and 1796 in French) is similar to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_and_Opinions_of_Tristram_Shandy,_Gentleman" title="The Life and Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman">Tristram Shandy</a></i> and <i>The Sentimental Journey</i> in its challenge to the conventional novel's structure and content.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="La_Religieuse_.28The_Nun_or_Memoirs_of_a_Nun.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="La_Religieuse_(The_Nun_or_Memoirs_of_a_Nun)"><i>La Religieuse</i> (<i>The Nun</i> or <i>Memoirs of a Nun</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: La Religieuse (The Nun or Memoirs of a Nun)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/La_Religieuse_(novel)" title="La Religieuse (novel)">La Religieuse</a></i> was a novel that claimed to show the corruption of the Catholic Church's institutions.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The novel began not as a work for literary consumption, but as an elaborate practical joke aimed at luring the <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine-Nicolas_de_Croismare" title="Marc-Antoine-Nicolas de Croismare">Marquis de Croismare</a>, a companion of Diderot's, back to Paris. <i>The Nun</i> is set in the Eighteenth century, that is, contemporary France. Suzanne Simonin is an intelligent and sensitive sixteen-year-old French girl who is forced against her will into a Catholic convent by her parents. Suzanne’s parents initially inform her that she is being sent to the convent for financial reasons. However, while in the convent, she learns that she is actually there because she is an illegitimate child, as her mother committed adultery. By sending Suzanne to the convent, her mother thought she could make amends for her sins by using her daughter as a sacrificial offering.
</p><p>At the convent, Suzanne suffers humiliation, harassment and violence because she refuses to make the vows of the religious community. She eventually finds companionship with the Mother Superior, Sister de Moni, who pities Suzanne’s anguish. After Sister de Moni's death, the new Mother Superior, Sister Sainte-Christine, does not share the same empathy for Suzanne that her predecessor had, blaming Suzanne for the death of Sister de Moni. Suzanne is physically and mentally harassed by Sister Sainte-Christine, almost to the point of death.
</p><p>Suzanne contacts her lawyer, Monsieur Manouri, who attempts to legally free her from her vows. Manouri manages to have Suzanne transferred to another convent, Sainte-Eutrope, . At the new convent, the Mother Superior is revealed to be a lesbian, and she grows affectionate towards Suzanne. The Mother Superior attempts to seduce Suzanne, but her innocence and chaste eventually drives the Mother Superior into insanity, leading to her death.
</p><p>Suzanne escapes the Sainte-Eutrope convent using the help of a priest. Following her liberation, she lives in fear of being captured and taken back to the convent as she awaits the help from Diderot's friend the <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine-Nicolas_de_Croismare" title="Marc-Antoine-Nicolas de Croismare">Marquis de Croismare</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Diderot was probably, but not a definite atheist; however, he did not use the novel as an outlet to condemn Christianity, but to show the corruption of the Catholic Church's institutions. In Diderot's telling, the Church fostered a hierarchical society, prevalent in the power dynamic between the Mother Superior and the girls in the convent. Girls were forced against their will to take their vows and endure the intolerable life of the convent. Diderot highlighted the victimization of women by the Catholic Church. Their subjection to the convent dehumanized them and represses their sexuality. Furthermore, the novel took place during a time in France when religious vows were regulated and enforced by the government. Through his cross-identification writing style, Diderot manifested the demeaning Catholic standards towards women that forced them to obey their determined fate under the hierarchical society.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poshumous_publication">Poshumous publication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Poshumous publication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although <i>The Nun</i> was completed in about 1780, the work was not published until 1796, after Diderot's death.
</p>
<h3><span id="Rameau.27s_Nephew"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rameau's_Nephew"><i>Rameau's Nephew</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rameau&#039;s Nephew">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Rameau%27s_Nephew" title="Rameau&#39;s Nephew">Rameau's Nephew</a></i> (French: <i>Le Neveu de Rameau</i>) is a "farce-tragedy" reminiscent of the <i>Satires</i> of <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>, a favorite classical author of Diderot's whose lines "Vertumnis, quotquot sunt, natus iniquis" ("Born under (the influence of) the unfavorable (gods) Vertumnuses, however many they are") appear as epigraph. According to Nicholas Cronk, <i>Rameau's Nephew</i> is "arguably the greatest work of the French Enlightenment's greatest writer."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg/300px-Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg/450px-Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg/600px-Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Un_d%C3%AEner_de_philosophes.Jean_Huber.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Un dîner de philosophes</i> painted by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Huber" title="Jean Huber">Jean Huber</a>. Denis Diderot is the second from the right (seated).</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Synopsis">Synopsis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Synopsis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The narrator in the book recounts a conversation with <a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Rameau" class="extiw" title="fr:Jean-François Rameau">Jean-François Rameau</a>, nephew of the famous <a href="/wiki/Jean-Philippe_Rameau" title="Jean-Philippe Rameau">Jean-Philippe Rameau</a>. The nephew composes and teaches music with some success but feels disadvantaged by his name and is jealous of his uncle. Eventually he sinks into an indolent and debauched state. After his wife's death, he loses all self-esteem and his brusque manners result in him being ostracized by former friends. A character profile of the nephew is now sketched by Diderot: a man who was once wealthy and comfortable with a pretty wife, who is now living in poverty and decadence, shunned by his friends. And yet this man retains enough of his past to analyze his despondency philosophically and maintains his sense of humor. Essentially he believes in nothing—not in religion, nor in morality; nor in the  Roussean view about nature being better than civilization since in his opinion every species in nature consumes one another.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>  He views the same process at work in the economic world where men consume each other through the legal system.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The wise man, according to the nephew, will consequently practice hedonism:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Hurrah for wisdom and philosophy!—the wisdom of Solomon: to drink good wines, gorge on choice foods, tumble pretty women, sleep on downy beds; outside of that, all is vanity.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_661_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_661-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The dialogue ends with Diderot calling the nephew a wastrel, a coward, and a glutton devoid of spiritual values to which the nephew replies: "I believe you are right."<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_661_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_661-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_2">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Diderot's intention in writing the dialogue—whether as a satire on contemporary manners, a reduction of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">self-interest</a> to an absurdity, the application of <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">irony</a> to the ethics of ordinary convention, a mere setting for a discussion about music, or a vigorous dramatic sketch of a parasite and a human original—is disputed. In political terms it explores "the bipolarisation of the social classes under absolute monarchy," and insofar as its protagonist demonstrates how the servant often manipulates the master, <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Neveu_de_Rameau" class="mw-redirect" title="Le Neveu de Rameau">Le Neveu de Rameau</a></i> can be seen to anticipate Hegel's <a href="/wiki/Master%E2%80%93slave_dialectic" title="Master–slave dialectic">master–slave dialectic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_publication">Posthumous publication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Posthumous publication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The publication history of the <i>Nephew</i> is circuitous. Written in 1761, Diderot never saw the work through to publication during his lifetime, and apparently did not even share it with his friends. After Diderot's death, a copy of the text reached <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Schiller</a>, who gave it to <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>, who, in 1805, translated the work into German.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley1911_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley1911-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Goethe's translation entered France, and was retranslated into French in 1821. Another copy of the text was published in 1823, but it had been expurgated by Diderot's daughter prior to publication. The original manuscript was only found in 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_659_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_659-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Diderot's most intimate friend was the <a href="/wiki/Philologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Philologist">philologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Melchior,_Baron_von_Grimm" title="Friedrich Melchior, Baron von Grimm">Friedrich Melchior Grimm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> They were brought together by their friend in common at that time, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_632_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_632-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 1753, Grimm began writing  a newsletter, the <i>La Correspondance littéraire, philosophique et critique</i>, which he would send to various high personages in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacobs_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1759, Grimm asked Diderot to report on the biennial art exhibitions in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> for the <i>Correspondance</i>. Diderot reported on the <a href="/wiki/Salon_(Paris)" title="Salon (Paris)">Salons</a> between 1759 and 1771 and again in 1775 and 1781.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Diderot's reports would become "the most celebrated contributions to La Correspondance."<sup id="cite_ref-Jacobs_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Augustin_Sainte-Beuve" title="Charles Augustin Sainte-Beuve">Charles Augustin Sainte-Beuve</a>, Diderot's reports initiated the French into a new way of laughing, and introduced people to the mystery and purport of colour by ideas. "Before Diderot", <a href="/wiki/Germaine_de_Sta%C3%ABl" title="Germaine de Staël">Anne Louise Germaine de Staël</a> wrote, "I had never seen anything in pictures except dull and lifeless colours; it was his imagination that gave them relief and life, and it is almost a new sense for which I am indebted to his genius".<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diderot had appended an <i>Essai sur la peinture</i> to his report on the 1765 Salon in which he expressed his views on artistic beauty. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a> described the <i>Essai sur la peinture</i> as "a magnificent work; it speaks even more usefully to the poet than to the painter, though for the painter too it is a torch of blazing illumination".<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_668_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_668-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Greuze" title="Jean-Baptiste Greuze">Jean-Baptiste Greuze</a> (1725–1805) was Diderot's favorite contemporary artist.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Diderot appreciated Greuze's sentimentality, and more particularly Greuze's portrayals of his wife who had once been Diderot's mistress.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_668_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_668-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatre">Theatre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Theatre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Diderot wrote sentimental plays, <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Fils_naturel" title="Le Fils naturel">Le Fils naturel</a></i> (1757) and <i><a href="/wiki/Le_P%C3%A8re_de_famille" title="Le Père de famille">Le Père de famille</a></i> (1758), accompanying them with essays on theatrical theory and practice, including "Les Entretiens sur <i>Le Fils Naturel</i>" (Conversations on <i>The Natural Son</i>), in which he announced the principles of a new drama: the 'serious genre', a realistic midpoint between comedy and tragedy that stood in opposition to the stilted conventions of the classical French stage.  In 1758, Diderot introduced the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_wall" title="Fourth wall">fourth wall</a>, the imaginary "wall" at the front of the stage in a traditional three-walled <a href="/wiki/Box_set_(theatre)" title="Box set (theatre)">box set</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Proscenium" title="Proscenium">proscenium</a> theatre, through which the audience sees the action in the world of the play.<sup id="cite_ref-Bell_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bell-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wallis_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallis-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abelman_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abelman-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diderot_and_Catherine_the_Great">Diderot and Catherine the Great</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Diderot and Catherine the Great">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Journey_to_Russia">Journey to Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Journey to Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg/300px-Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg/450px-Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg/600px-Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1240" data-file-height="860" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diderot%27s_travel_from_Paris_to_Saint_Petersburg_in_1773-1774_map-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diderot's travel from Paris to Saint Petersburg in 1773–1774. The <b>blue line</b> marks the outward from 3 June 1773 until 9 October 1773, and the <b>red line</b> marks the return journey 5 March 1774 to 21 October 1774.</div></div></div>
<p>When the Russian Empress <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> heard that Diderot was in need of money, she arranged to buy his library and appoint him caretaker of it until his death, at a salary of  1,000 livres per year. She even paid him 50 years salary in advance.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Although Diderot hated traveling,<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_674_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_674-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> he was obliged to visit her.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_448_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_448-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 9 October 1773, he reached St. Petersburg, met Catherine the next day and they had several discussions on various subjects. During his five-month stay at her court, he met her almost every day.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> During these conversations, he would later state, they spoke 'man to man'.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_448_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_448-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He would occasionally make his point by slapping her thighs. In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Marie_Th%C3%A9r%C3%A8se_Rodet_Geoffrin" title="Marie Thérèse Rodet Geoffrin">Madame Geoffrin</a>, Catherine wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Your Diderot is an extraordinary man. I emerge from interviews with him with my thighs bruised and quite black. I have been obliged to put a table between us to protect myself and my members.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_448_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_448-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>One of the topics discussed was Diderot's ideas about how to transform Russia into a utopia. In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe,_comte_de_S%C3%A9gur" title="Louis Philippe, comte de Ségur">Comte de Ségur</a>, the Empress wrote that if she followed Diderot's advice, chaos would ensue in her kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_448_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_448-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Back_in_France">Back in France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Back in France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When returning, Diderot asked the Empress for 1,500 rubles as reimbursement for his trip. She gave him 3,000 rubles, an expensive ring, and an officer to escort him back to Paris. He would write a eulogy in her honor on reaching Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_449_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_449-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1766, when Catherine heard that Diderot had not received his annual fee for editing the <i>Encyclopédie</i> (an important source of income for the philosopher), she arranged for him to receive a massive sum of 50,000 livres as an advance for his services as her librarian.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 1784, upon hearing that Diderot was in poor health, Catherine arranged for him to move into a luxurious suite in the <i>Rue de Richelieu</i>. Diderot died two weeks after moving there—on 31 July 1784.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among Diderot's last works were notes "On the Instructions of her Imperial Majesty...for the Drawing up of Laws". This commentary on Russia included replies to some arguments Catherine had made in the <a href="/wiki/Nakaz" title="Nakaz">Nakaz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_449_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_449-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_393_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_393-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Diderot wrote that Catherine was certainly despotic, due to circumstances and training, but was not inherently tyrannical. Thus, if she wished to destroy despotism in Russia, she should abdicate her throne and destroy anyone who tries to revive the monarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_393_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_393-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> She should publicly declare that "there is no true sovereign other than the nation, and there can be no true legislator other than the people."<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_394_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_394-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> She should create a new Russian legal code establishing an independent legal framework and starting with the text: "We the people, and we the sovereign of this people, swear conjointly these laws, by which we are judged equally."<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_394_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_394-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In the <i>Nakaz</i>, Catherine had written: "It is for legislation to follow the spirit of the nation."<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_394_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_394-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Diderot's rebuttal stated that it is for legislation to <i>make</i> the spirit of the nation. For instance, he argued, it is not appropriate to make public executions unnecessarily horrific.<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_394-5_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_394-5-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultimately, Diderot decided not to send these notes to Catherine; however, they were delivered to her with his other papers after he died. When she read them, she was furious and commented that they were an incoherent gibberish devoid of prudence, insight, and verisimilitude.<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1967_449_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1967_449-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_395_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_395-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In his youth, Diderot was originally a follower of <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a> <i>Anglomanie</i>, but gradually moved away from this line of thought towards <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>, a move which was finally realised in 1747 in the philosophical debate in the second part of his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Skeptic%27s_Walk" title="The Skeptic&#39;s Walk">The Skeptic's Walk</a></i> (1747).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Diderot opposed mysticism and occultism, which were highly prevalent in France at the time he wrote, and believed religious truth claims must fall under the domain of reason, not mystical experience or esoteric secrets. However, Diderot showed some interest in the work of <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> He was "a philosopher in whom all the contradictions of the time struggle with one another" (<a href="/wiki/Johann_Karl_Friedrich_Rosenkranz" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann Karl Friedrich Rosenkranz">Rosenkranz</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley1911_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley1911-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
In his 1754 book <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_interpretation_of_Nature" title="On the interpretation of Nature">On the interpretation of Nature</a></i>, Diderot expounded on his views about Nature, evolution, materialism, mathematics, and experimental science.<sup id="cite_ref-Durant_1965_651-2_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durant_1965_651-2-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_109-115_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_109-115-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It is speculated that Diderot may have contributed to his friend <a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d&#39;Holbach">Baron d'Holbach</a>'s 1770 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_System_of_Nature" title="The System of Nature">The System of Nature</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley1911_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley1911-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Diderot had enthusiastically endorsed the book stating that: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>What I like is a philosophy clear, definite, and frank, such as you have in the <i>System of Nature</i>. The author is not an atheist on one page and a deist on another. His philosophy is all of one piece.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In conceiving the <i>Encyclopédie</i>, Diderot had thought of the work as a fight on behalf of posterity and had expressed confidence that posterity would be grateful for his effort. According to Diderot, "posterity is for the philosopher what the 'other world' is for the man of religion."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_burial">Death and burial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Death and burial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Diderot died of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary thrombosis</a> in Paris on 31 July 1784, and was buried in the city's <a href="/wiki/Saint-Roch,_Paris" title="Saint-Roch, Paris">Église Saint-Roch</a>. His heirs sent his vast library to Catherine II, who had it deposited at the <a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Russia" title="National Library of Russia">National Library of Russia</a>. He has several times been denied burial in the <a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on,_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Panthéon, Paris">Panthéon</a> with other French notables.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The French government considered memorializing him in this fashion on the 300th anniversary of his birth,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> but this did not come to pass.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appreciation_and_influence">Appreciation and influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Appreciation and influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg/220px-Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg/330px-Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg/440px-Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1679" data-file-height="2126" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy_-_Bildnis_eines_Herrn_mit_der_B%C3%BCste_des_Denis_Diderot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy" title="Jean-Simon Berthélemy">Jean-Simon Berthélemy</a>, Young man admiring Denis Diderot's bust</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Marmontel" title="Jean-François Marmontel">Marmontel</a> and <a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacques-Henri_Meister" class="extiw" title="fr:Jacques-Henri Meister">Henri Meister</a> commented on the great pleasure of having intellectual conversations with Diderot.<sup id="cite_ref-Durant_1965_678_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durant_1965_678-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Morellet" title="André Morellet">Morellet</a>, a regular attendee at <a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach#D&#39;Holbach&#39;s_salon" title="Baron d&#39;Holbach">D'Holbach's salon</a>, wrote: "It is there that I heard...Diderot treat questions of philosophy, art, or literature, and by his wealth of expression, fluency, and inspired appearance, hold our attention for a long stretch of time."<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_1972_175_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson_1972_175-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Diderot's contemporary, and rival, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean Jacques Rousseau</a> wrote in his <i>Confessions</i> that after a few centuries Diderot would be accorded as much respect by posterity as was given to Plato and Aristotle.<sup id="cite_ref-Durant_1965_678_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durant_1965_678-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In Germany, <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schiller" class="mw-redirect" title="Schiller">Schiller</a>, and Lessing<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> expressed admiration for Diderot's writings, Goethe pronouncing Diderot's <i><a href="/wiki/Rameau%27s_Nephew" title="Rameau&#39;s Nephew">Rameau's Nephew</a></i> to be "the classical work of an outstanding man" and that "Diderot is Diderot, a unique individual; whoever carps at him and his affairs is a philistine."<sup id="cite_ref-Will_Durant_1965_659_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will_Durant_1965_659-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the next century, Diderot was admired by <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_de_Balzac" title="Honoré de Balzac">Balzac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Delacroix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stendhal" title="Stendhal">Stendhal</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola" title="Émile Zola">Zola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schopenhauer" class="mw-redirect" title="Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  According to <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Comte</a>, Diderot was the foremost intellectual in an exciting age.<sup id="cite_ref-Age_of_Voltaire_679_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Voltaire_679-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>  Historian <a href="/wiki/Jules_Michelet" title="Jules Michelet">Michelet</a> described him as "the true Prometheus" and stated that Diderot's ideas would continue to remain influential long into the future. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a> chose Diderot as his "favourite prose-writer."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_tributes">Contemporary tributes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Contemporary tributes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diderot-statue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Diderot-statue.jpg/220px-Diderot-statue.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Diderot-statue.jpg/330px-Diderot-statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Diderot-statue.jpg/440px-Diderot-statue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diderot-statue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monument to Denis Diderot in Paris, <a href="/wiki/6th_arrondissement_of_Paris" title="6th arrondissement of Paris">6th arrondissement</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Gautherin" title="Jean Gautherin">Jean Gautherin</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Otis_Fellows" title="Otis Fellows">Otis Fellows</a> and Norman Torrey have described Diderot as "the most interesting and provocative figure of the French eighteenth century."<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1993, American writer Cathleen Schine published <i>Rameau's Niece</i>, a satire of academic life in New York that took as its premise a woman's research into an (imagined) 18th-century pornographic parody of Diderot's <i>Rameau's Nephew</i>. The book was praised by Michiko Kakutani in the <i>New York Times</i> as "a nimble philosophical satire of the academic mind" and "an enchanting comedy of modern manners."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>French author Eric-Emmanuel Schmitt wrote a play titled <i>Le Libertin</i> (<i>The Libertine</i>) which imagines a day in Diderot's life including a fictional sitting for a woman painter which becomes sexually charged but is interrupted by the demands of editing the <i>Encyclopédie</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> It was first staged at Paris' Théâtre Montparnasse in 1997 starring Bernard Giraudeau as Diderot and Christiane Cohendy as Madame Therbouche and was well received by critics.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, the tricentennial of Diderot's birth, his hometown of Langres held a series of events in his honor and produced an audio tour of the town highlighting places that were part of Diderot's past, including the remains of the convent where his sister Angélique took her vows.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> On 6 October 2013, a museum of the Enlightenment focusing on Diderot's contributions to the movement, the  <a href="/wiki/Denis_Diderot_House_Of_Enlightenment_(France)" class="mw-redirect" title="Denis Diderot House Of Enlightenment (France)">Maison des Lumières Denis Diderot</a>, was inaugurated in Langres.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denis_Diderot&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Bijou</i> is a slang word meaning the vagina.<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_44_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_44-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Diderot later narrated the following conversation as having taken place:

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>Catherine</i>: "You have a hot head, and I have one too. We interrupt each other, we do not hear what the other one says, and so we say stupid things."
</p><p><i>Diderot</i>: "With this difference, that when I interrupt your Majesty, I commit a great impertinence."
</p><p><i>Catherine</i>: "No, between men there is no such thing as impertinence."<sup id="cite_ref-Furbank_1992_379_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Furbank_1992_379-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>  </span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Diderot's contemporary, also a Frenchman, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Louis_Maupertuis" title="Pierre Louis Maupertuis">Pierre Louis Maupertuis</a>—who in 1745 was named Head of the Prussian Academy of Science under <a href="/wiki/Frederic_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederic the Great">Frederic the Great</a>—was developing similar ideas. These proto-evolutionary theories were by no means as thought out and systematic as those of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> a hundred years later.</span>
